        <meta property="og:url" content="https://pubs.acs.org/doi/full/10.1021/acs.jpclett.2c03061"><meta property="og:title" content="Bonding Heterogeneity Leads to Hierarchical and Ultralow Lattice Thermal Conductivity in Sodium Metavanadate"><meta property="og:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acs.jpclett.2c03061/asset/images/acs.jpclett.2c03061.social.jpeg_v03"><meta property="og:description" content="Sodium metavanadate (NaVO3) is a promising low-cost candidate as a cathode material for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) owing to its high cycle performance. Its thermal transport, although being a central factor limiting its practical applications, remains scarce. Herein, we comprehensively investigate the intrinsic thermal transport properties of the two phases of NaVO3 using the unified theory. Importantly, we identify a hierarchical thermal transport mechanism in NaVO3 and the significant contribution of diffusive thermal transport. With the combined two channels, we reveal that NaVO3 has the anisotropic and ultralow thermal conductivity κ, which is derived from the bonding heterogeneity with the coexistence of strong V–O bonds and weak Na–O bonds, implying the possibility of engineering the κ of SIBs by spatially tuning the sodium concentration distribution. Our work establishes a fundamental understanding of the intrinsic thermal transport of NaVO3 and provides guidance toward designing tunable thermal conductivity cathode materials for SIBs."><meta property="og:type" content="Article"><meta property="og:site_name" content="ACS Publications"><meta name="twitter:card" content="summary_large_image"><meta name="twitter:site" content="@ACSPublications"><meta name="twitter:image" content="https://pubs.acs.org/cms/10.1021/acs.jpclett.2c03061/asset/images/acs.jpclett.2c03061.social.jpeg_v03">

<!-- loading Javascript for using the persistence Service -->
    <script src="/wro/1122814~thiss-ds.js" async="" nonce="93187dcd8d35075b-SJC"></script>

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="93187dcd8d35075b-SJC">

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            timeout: 5000,

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
            },
            retrieveEntity: function (ms) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                if (ms == undefined || ms === '') {
                    ms = this.timeout;
                }

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res.data);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('failed to retrieve entity from local storage: ' + err);
                        });
                    // Set up the timeout
                    setTimeout(function () {
                        reject('Promise timed out ' + ms + ' ms');
                    }, ms);
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    persistService.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage due to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {
                            doi: doi,
                            entityid: entityId,
                            entityTitle: entityTitle,
                            eventGroupKey: 'b5fe69d8-3ef6-44d9-aa76-937fc0e65c20'
                        }

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    }).fail(function (status) {
                        return reject(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (i = 0; i < allSsoInstitutions.length; i++) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        if(allSsoInstitutions.length === 0 && true){
                            self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, null, null)
                                .then(function () {
                                    console.log('No Entity found');
                                }, function (err) {
                                    console.log('failed to get the none entity status' ,err);
                                });
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            }).catch(function (err) {
                                reject(err);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();

                    if ($('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').length > 0) {
                        let institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox = $('.institution-preference-userconsent-checkbox').get(0);
                        if (!$(institutionPreferenceUserConsentCheckbox).is(":checked")) {
                            return;
                        }
                    }

                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

<script type="text/javascript" nonce="93187dcd8d35075b-SJC">
var dfpReady = false;
$(document).ready(function() {
    // var journalTitle = 'physical_chemistry_letter';
    var taxonomyKeywords = $('.article_header-taxonomy');
    var articleKeyword = $('meta[name="keywords"]');
    var articleTitle = $('meta[name="dc.Title"]');
    var articleDescription = $('meta[name="dc.Description"]');
    var abstractKeyword = $('p.articleBody_abstractText:contains("Keywords: ")');
    var searchTerms = [];

    if (taxonomyKeywords.length > 0) {
        $(taxonomyKeywords).children(".rlist--inline").children("li").each(function() {
            searchTerms.push($(this).text());
        });
    }
    else if (articleKeyword.length > 0) { 
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleKeyword.prop('content').split(', '));
    }
    else if (abstractKeyword.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(abstractKeyword.text().split("Keywords: ")[1].split('; '));
    }
    else if (articleDescription.length > 0) {
        $(articleDescription).each(function() {
            articleDescriptionKeywords = $(this).prop('content');
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/.,/g,"");
            articleDescriptionKeywords = articleDescriptionKeywords.replace(/\s{2,}/g," ");
            searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleDescriptionKeywords.split(' '));
        });
    }

    if (articleTitle.length > 0) {
        searchTerms = searchTerms.concat(articleTitle.prop('content').split(' '));
    }

    googletag.cmd.push(function() {
        var mappingLeader = googletag.sizeMapping().
        addSize([750, 200], [728, 90]). 
        addSize([0, 0], [320, 50]).
        build();
        //leaderboard
        googletag.defineSlot('/8868/journal/physical_chemistry_letter/article', [728, 90], 'dfp-journal-article-leaderboard').defineSizeMapping(mappingLeader).setTargeting('location', 'leaderboard1').setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //recommend
        googletag.defineSlot("/8868/journal/physical_chemistry_letter/article", [1, 1], "dfp-journal-article-recommend").setTargeting("ntvPlacement", ["1100167"]).setTargeting("location", "recommend").setTargeting('Keyword', searchTerms).addService(googletag.pubads());
        //googletag.pubads().enableSingleRequest();
        googletag.pubads().collapseEmptyDivs();
        googletag.pubads().setTargeting('inst',dartInstitutionType).setTargeting('memb',dartMember);
        googletag.pubads().addEventListener("slotRenderEnded", function(event) {
            console.log(event.slot.getSlotElementId() + " isEmpty: " + event.isEmpty + " | creative id: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            if((event.isEmpty === false) && (event.slot.getSlotElementId() != "dfp-journal-article-recommend")) {
                $("#" + event.slot.getSlotElementId()).parents(".advertisement").slideDown();
                console.log("ad display: " + event.creativeId + " " + event.size[0] + "x" + event.size[1]);
            }
            // else {
            //     console.log("no ad found");
            // }
        });
        googletag.enableServices();
        dfpReady = true;
    });
});
</script>

data-references="https://pubs.acs.org/action/ajaxShowEnhancedAbstract?doi=10.1021%2Facs.jpclett.2c03061&amp;ajax=true" data-enable-mathjax="true" vocab="http://schema.org/" typeof="ScholarlyArticle" class="article--latest"><div class="figure-viewer"><div class="figure-viewer_row"><div class="figure-viewer_col loop-trap"><div aria-live="polite" aria-atomic="true" class="figure-viewer_modal"><a <script nonce="93187dcd8d35075b-SJC">
$(function() {
    var serialCode = "jpclcd";
    var books = ["reagents", "symposium", "advances", "medichemreviews", "styleguide"]
    if(books.includes(serialCode) === false) {
        // default click action
        $("[data-id]").on("mousedown", function(e) {
            var currentClickID = $(this).attr("data-id");

            if(currentClickID.includes("_hover") === false) {
                if(currentClickID.match("^article_") && !currentClickID.match("^article_abstract_CC")) {   
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Bonding Heterogeneity Leads to Hierarchical and Ultralow Lattice Thermal Conductivity in Sodium Metavanadate", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.jpclett.2c03061", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                }
            }
        });
        
        // Access Management icons hover
        var articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        var articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent === false) {
                articleAccessManagementIconHover = true;
                var articleAccessManagementHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleAccessManagementIconHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Bonding Heterogeneity Leads to Hierarchical and Ultralow Lattice Thermal Conductivity in Sodium Metavanadate", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.jpclett.2c03061", "Article_Element": articleAccessManagementHover });
    
                        articleAccessManagementIconHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_access_icon']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAccessManagementIconHover = false;
        });
    
        // articleRightsPermissionsLink hover
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        var articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = false;
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent === false) {
                articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = true;
                var articleRightsHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if(articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover === true) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Bonding Heterogeneity Leads to Hierarchical and Ultralow Lattice Thermal Conductivity in Sodium Metavanadate", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.jpclett.2c03061", "Article_Element": articleRightsHover });
    
                        articleRightsPermissionsLinkHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_rights_permissions']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleRightsPermissionsLinkHover = false;
        });
    
        // Author hover
        var articleAuthorHover = false
        var articleAuthorHoverEvent = false
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if((articleAuthorHoverEvent === false) && ($(this).parent().hasClass("view-affs") === false)) {
                articleAuthorHover = true;
                var authorInfoHover =  $(this).attr("data-id") + "_hover";
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAuthorHover === true) && (articleAuthorHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Bonding Heterogeneity Leads to Hierarchical and Ultralow Lattice Thermal Conductivity in Sodium Metavanadate", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.jpclett.2c03061", "Article_Element": authorInfoHover });
    
                        articleAuthorHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $("[data-id='article_author_info']").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            articleAuthorHover = false;
        });
    
        // CC License hover
        var articleAbstractCCHover = false
        var articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = false
        var articleDrawerCCHover = false
        var articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = false
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseenter", function(e) {
            if($(this).attr("data-id")) {
                if(($(this).attr("data-id") === "article_drawer_CC-License") && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleDrawerCCHover = true;
                    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleDrawerCCHover === true) && (articleDrawerCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Bonding Heterogeneity Leads to Hierarchical and Ultralow Lattice Thermal Conductivity in Sodium Metavanadate", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.jpclett.2c03061", "Article_Element": "article_drawer_CC-License_hover" });
    
                            articleDrawerCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
                else if(($(this).attr("data-id").match("^article_abstract_CC")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                    articleAbstractCCHover = true;
    
                    setTimeout(function() { 
                        if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                            gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Bonding Heterogeneity Leads to Hierarchical and Ultralow Lattice Thermal Conductivity in Sodium Metavanadate", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.jpclett.2c03061", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                            articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                        }
                    }, 1000);
                }
            }
            else if(($(this).hasClass("article__cc-license__link")) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = true;
                
                setTimeout(function() { 
                    if((articleAbstractCCHover === true) && (articleAbstractCCHoverEvent === false)) {
                        gtag("event", "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Bonding Heterogeneity Leads to Hierarchical and Ultralow Lattice Thermal Conductivity in Sodium Metavanadate", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.jpclett.2c03061", "Article_Element": "article_abstract_CC_hover" });
    
                        articleAbstractCCHoverEvent = true;
                    }
                }, 1000);
            }
        });
        $(".article__cc-license__inter a").on("mouseleave", function(e) {
            if(articleAbstractCCHover === true) {
                articleAbstractCCHover = false;
            }
            if(articleDrawerCCHover = true) {
                articleDrawerCCHover = false;
            }
        });
        
        // show recommeneded click delay
        var intervalCount = 0;
        var showRecommendedInterval = setInterval(function() {
            intervalCount += 100;
            if($(".show-recommended__wrapper .show-recommended").length > 0) {
                clearInterval(showRecommendedInterval);
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowMore']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Bonding Heterogeneity Leads to Hierarchical and Ultralow Lattice Thermal Conductivity in Sodium Metavanadate", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.jpclett.2c03061", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
                $("[data-id='article_drawer_RecommendedShowLess']").on("mousedown", function(e) {
                    gtag('event', "Journal Article", { "Article_Title": "Bonding Heterogeneity Leads to Hierarchical and Ultralow Lattice Thermal Conductivity in Sodium Metavanadate", "Article_DOI": "10.1021/acs.jpclett.2c03061", "Article_Element": $(this).attr("data-id") });
                });
            }
        }, 100);
    }
});
</script>

class="articleMetrics_count"><h2>Citations</h2><div class="articleMetrics-val"><a href="#citeThis" class="internalNav">9</a></div></div></div></div><div class="articleMetrics_about"><div class="base data-db-target-of="aboutArticleMetrics" id="about-article-metrics" class="dropBlock__holder"><button class="articleMetrics_close"> <i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-close_thin"></i><span Find more information on<a href="https://help.altmetric.com/support/solutions/articles/6000233311-how-is-the-altmetric-attention-score-calculated"> the Altmetric Attention Score and how the score is calculated.</a></p></div></div></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="article_abstract"><div class="article_abstract-title" id="Abstract" tabindex="0"><h2>Abstract</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div><div class="article_abstract-content hlFld-Abstract" id="abstractBox" property="description"><figure id="_i1" data-index="0" class="article__inlineFigure article_abstract-img"><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img class="inline-fig internalNav" src="/cms/10.1021/acs.jpclett.2c03061/asset/images/medium/jz2c03061_0006.gif" alt="" id="tgr1" property="image" style="width:405px; height:275px"></button><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.jpclett.2c03061/asset/images/large/jz2c03061_0006.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.jpclett.2c03061&amp;id=_i1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p class="articleBody_abstractText">Sodium metavanadate (NaVO<sub>3</sub>) is a promising low-cost candidate as a cathode material for sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) owing to its high cycle performance. Its thermal transport, although being a central factor limiting its practical applications, remains scarce. Herein, we comprehensively investigate the intrinsic thermal transport properties of the two phases of NaVO<sub>3</sub> using the unified theory. Importantly, we identify a hierarchical thermal transport mechanism in NaVO<sub>3</sub> and the significant contribution of diffusive thermal transport. With the combined two channels, we reveal that NaVO<sub>3</sub> has the anisotropic and ultralow thermal conductivity κ, which is derived from the bonding heterogeneity with the coexistence of strong V–O bonds and weak Na–O bonds, implying the possibility of engineering the κ of SIBs by spatially tuning the sodium concentration distribution. Our work establishes a fundamental understanding of the intrinsic thermal transport of NaVO<sub>3</sub> and provides guidance toward designing tunable thermal conductivity cathode materials for SIBs.</p></div><div class="article__cc-license"><div class="article__cc-license__body"><p class="article__cc-license__para">This publication is licensed under the terms of your
                            institutional subscription.
                            <a href="/servlet/linkout?type=rightslink&amp;url=startPage%3D11160%26pageCount%3D9%26copyright%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26author%3DXin%2BJin%252C%2BXianyong%2BDing%252C%2BFangyang%2BZhan%252C%2Bet%2Bal%26orderBeanReset%3Dtrue%26imprint%3DAmerican%2BChemical%2BSociety%26volumeNum%3D13%26issueNum%3D48%26contentID%3Dacs.jpclett.2c03061%26title%3DBonding%2BHeterogeneity%2BLeads%2Bto%2BHierarchical%2Band%2BUltralow%2BLattice%2BThermal%2BConductivity%2Bin%2BSodium%2BMetavanadate%26numPages%3D9%26pa%3D%26issn%3D1948-7185%26publisherName%3Dacs%26publication%3Djpclcd%26rpt%3Dn%26endPage%3D11168%26publicationDate%3DDecember%2B2022" class="link--bold">Request reuse permissions.</a></p></div></div><span class="hidden" property="publisher">ACS Publications</span><div class="article_header-article-copyright">Copyright © 2022 American Chemical Society</div><div class="article_header-footer-right"><div class="articleHeaderDropzone7" data-pb-dropzone="articleHeaderDropzone7"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div></div></div><div class="article_content"><div class="article_content-table hlFld-FullText"><div class="article_content-row"><div class="article_content-left" property="articleBody" style="width: 384px;"><p class="first">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have been recognized as the promising electrode candidates as compared to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs), largely owing to the low cost and earth-abundance of sodium. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref1 ref2 ref3" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref1 ref2 ref3" aria-label="reference 1 to 3">(1−3)</a> As a typical representative of SIBs, sodium metavanadate (NaVO<sub>3</sub>) has gained considerable attention due to its high cycle performance with high capacity as a cathode material. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref4 ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref4 ref5 ref6 ref7 ref8" aria-label="reference 4 to 8">(4−8)</a> Benefiting from the layered crystal structure, NaVO<sub>3</sub> can store energy by Na<sup>+</sup> insertion/extraction through charge compensation from V cations. It has been experimentally <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref9" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref9" aria-label="reference 9">(9)</a> shown that, unlike other sodium-intercalated vanadium oxides, NaVO<sub>3</sub> is able to provide a fast sodium ion diffusion channel without involving significant irreversible structural distortions during charge and discharge, thereby hindering the fast capacity fading. This superiority allows NaVO<sub>3</sub> to have a higher reversible capacity than other sodium-intercalated vanadium oxides. However, achieving its practical application in large-scale energy storage is rather difficult, in fact, given that the performance of a battery material is always limited by many highly coupled properties. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref10 ref11 ref12 ref13" aria-label="reference 10 to 13">(10−13)</a> Among various constraints, thermal transport property is one of the central bottlenecks in many applications as it limits the charging/discharging rate, package density, energy efficiency, cycle lifetime, and safety of batteries. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13 ref14" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref13 ref14" aria-label="reference 13 and 14">(13,14)</a> The heat is generated either when current flows through the internal resistance of the battery or when chemical reactions take place during cycling. Once the heat cannot be dissipated from the battery in time, it will cause overheating inside the battery device, which may cause a catastrophic destruction of the battery. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref14 ref15" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref14 ref15" aria-label="reference 14 and 15">(14,15)</a> Therefore, enabling the efficient thermal management is of great importance for practical applications of batteries. In this context, several attempts <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref13 ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref13 ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19" aria-label="reference 13 and 16 to 19">(13,16−19)</a> have been made to highlight the importance of understanding thermal transport of electrode materials, not only for managing heat generation and dissipation in LIBs but also for operando probing of the local lithium concentration. However, the fundamental understanding of the intrinsic thermal transport in SIBs and the underlying physical mechanisms are still lacking.</p><p>Despite its significance in practical applications of SIBs, investigation of the thermal transport properties of NaVO<sub>3</sub> is actually a nontrivial task, since the conventional Peierls–Boltzmann transport equation (PBTE) based particle-like treatment of phonons is insufficient in describing the thermal transport behavior of crystalline compounds with complicated crystal structures like NaVO<sub>3</sub> or strong anharmonicity. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20 ref21 ref22 ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref20 ref21 ref22 ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27" aria-label="reference 20 to 27">(20−27)</a> The failure of PBTE in terms of describing the lattice dynamics of these complex structural materials stems from its inability to properly treat phonons with mean free path (MFP) less than the interatomic distance, the so-called Ioffe–Regel limit. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref28" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref28" aria-label="reference 28">(28)</a> Recently, many efforts have been made to tackle this drawback by developing unified theories and numerical methods beyond the PBTE scheme. Notably, the recent advance in thermal transport theories and methods have enabled the consideration of contributions arising from the particle-like propagation as well as wave-like diffusons. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21 ref27 ref29 ref30 ref31" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref21 ref27 ref29 ref30 ref31" aria-label="reference 21, 27 and 29 to 31">(21,27,29−31)</a> For instance, Mukhopadhyay et al. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a> used the minimum of interatomic spacing as the Ioffe–Regel criterion and proposed a phenomenological two-channel thermal transport model in which the phonon modes with long MFP are treated by a standard phonon channel using PBTE whereas those with small MFP are described by a hopping channel using Einstein’s or Cahill’s model, which gave predictions of κ and its temperature (<i>T</i>) dependence in reasonable agreement with measurements in crystalline Tl<sub>3</sub>VSe<sub>4</sub>. Later, Simoncelli et al. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> derived a transport equation based on the phonon velocity operator with diagonal and off-diagonal elements describing propagative and diffusive thermal transport contributions, respectively, which was validated in CsPbBr<sub>3</sub>. Meanwhile, Isaeva et al. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref30" aria-label="reference 30">(30)</a> developed a quasi-harmonic Green–Kubo method that unifies the PBTE approach within the single-mode relaxation-time approximation (RTA) for crystals and a generalization of the Allen–Feldman model for glasses. Subsequently, Luo et al. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref27" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref27" aria-label="reference 27">(27)</a> proposed a vibrational hierarchy-based dual-phonon transport theory by treating the short-MFP phonons with the diffusion theory and other normal phonons with the PBTE, and they successfully explained the experimentally observed thermal conductivity of La<sub>2</sub>Zr<sub>2</sub>O<sub>7</sub> and its temperature dependence. The establishment of these theories or methods indicates that the research techniques of predicting thermal transport properties of complex crystalline materials, as we show here in NaVO<sub>3</sub>, have been gradually maturing.</p><p>In this work, we employ the unified theory of lattice heat transport developed by Simoncelli et al. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref29" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref29" aria-label="reference 29">(29)</a> combined with first-principles calculations to investigate the thermal transport properties of NaVO<sub>3</sub>. We explicitly reveal the hierarchical and ultralow lattice thermal conductivity of NaVO<sub>3</sub>, and we find that diffusive transport channels have a significant contribution at higher temperatures, giving rise to the much weaker κ ∼ <i>T</i> trend than the typical <i>T</i><sup>−1</sup> behavior in crystals. To reveal the origin of hierarchical transport behavior in NaVO<sub>3</sub>, a detailed analysis of lattice structure, phonon group velocities, phonon scattering rates, scattering phase space, phonon anharmonicity, and electronic properties is presented. Importantly, we identify that this unusual behavior stems from the strong phonon anharmonicity and a large amount of three-phonon scattering phase space, which is strongly correlated with the bonding heterogeneity as revealed by the projected crystal orbital Hamiltan population (pCOHP) and electron localization function (ELF). This work fills the gap in the theoretical study of the lattice dynamics and thermal transport of NaVO<sub>3</sub>, and it might have strong impacts on the thermal management of SIBs.</p><p>All the first-principles calculations are carried out by density functional theory employing the projector augmented wave (PAW) pseudopotential in the Vienna ab initio simulation package (VASP) code. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref32 ref33" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref32 ref33" aria-label="reference 32 and 33">(32,33)</a> The exchange–correlation functional adopts the Perdew–Burke–Ernzerhof (PBE) generalized gradient approximation (GGA). <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref34" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref34" aria-label="reference 34">(34)</a> The plane wave cutoff energy is set to 520 eV and the reciprocal space is determined by the Monkhorst–Pack scheme <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref35" aria-label="reference 35">(35)</a> with mesh size of 6 × 6 × 7 and 3 × 15 × 9 for α-NaVO<sub>3</sub> and β-NaVO<sub>3</sub>, respectively. The energy and force convergence criteria are set to 10<sup>–8</sup> eV and 10<sup>–3</sup> eV/Å, respectively. The harmonic second-order force constants and phonon spectra are computed by using VASP and PHONOPY <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref36" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref36" aria-label="reference 36">(36)</a> software with 2 × 2 × 3 supercells via the finite displacement method. The nonanalytical corrections are applied to the dynamical matrix to account for the long-range electrostatic interactions. The anharmonic third-order force constants are computed by the third-order script, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref37" aria-label="reference 37">(37)</a> wherein the interatomic interactions are considered up to the sixth nearest neighbors with 2 × 2 × 2 supercells. The thermal conductivity calculation is implemented in the modified in-house ShengBTE package, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref37 ref38 ref39" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref37 ref38 ref39" aria-label="reference 37 to 39">(37−39)</a> with the detailed expressions given in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.jpclett.2c03061/suppl_file/jz2c03061_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>. For achieving the convergence of thermal conductivity, the <i>q</i>-grid mesh is set to 10 × 10 × 10 and 4 × 20 × 16 for α-NaVO<sub>3</sub> and β-NaVO<sub>3</sub>, respectively.</p><p>Sodium metavanadates are framework structure crystals with networks formed by V–O chains, and they have different phases. In this work, we consider both the monoclinic phase with space group <i>Cc</i> (α-NaVO<sub>3</sub>) and orthorhombic phase with space group <i>Pnma</i> (β-NaVO<sub>3</sub>). The crystal structures of these two phases are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>a,b. α-NaVO<sub>3</sub> crystal has a layered structure with 20 atoms in the primitive cell, consisting of O–V–O slabs made of chains of VO<sub>4</sub> tetrahedra vertex- or point-shared with two neighboring VO<sub>4</sub> tetrahedra. β-NaVO<sub>3</sub> also contains 20 atoms in the primitive cell, where the infinite double metavanadate chains run parallel to the <i>a</i>-axis and are bound together by Na ions. Within a chain, each V atom is surrounded by five O atoms to form a distorted trigonal bipyramid. The optimized lattice constants <i>a</i>, <i>b</i>, and <i>c</i> for two phases are provided in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.jpclett.2c03061/suppl_file/jz2c03061_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Table S1</a>, which are in good agreement with experiments. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref40 ref41" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref40 ref41" aria-label="reference 40 and 41">(40,41)</a></p><figure id="fig1" data-index="1" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 1</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.jpclett.2c03061/asset/images/medium/jz2c03061_0001.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.jpclett.2c03061/asset/images/large/jz2c03061_0001.jpeg" id="gr1" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 1. Conventional crystal structure of (a) α-NaVO<sub>3</sub> and (b) β-NaVO<sub>3</sub>. Phonon dispersion curves along high-symmetry directions of the Brillouin zone and corresponding phonon density of states of (c) α-NaVO<sub>3</sub> and (d) β-NaVO<sub>3</sub>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.jpclett.2c03061/asset/images/large/jz2c03061_0001.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.jpclett.2c03061&amp;id=fig1"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p>Parts c and d of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a> present the calculated phonon dispersion curves together with the projected phonon density of states (PDOS) of α-NaVO<sub>3</sub> and β-NaVO<sub>3</sub>. The absence of any imaginary frequency modes confirms the dynamic stability of these phases. The primitive cell of these two phases contain 20 atoms; therefore, there are three acoustic and 57 optical branches in their phonon dispersions. As observed in their phonon dispersions, there exists a severe overlap between the acoustic and the optical phonon modes in the low-frequency region below 5 THz, which can provide large three-phonon scattering channels for heat-carrying acoustic phonons, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref24 ref27" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref24 ref27" aria-label="reference 24 and 27">(24,27)</a> as confirmed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>c. We also notice that several optical branches near the frequency range of 20–25 THz for α-NaVO<sub>3</sub> and from 15 to 20 THz for β-NaVO<sub>3</sub> are rather dispersive, distinct from the flat feature of other optical curves, which implies that these modes possess large phonon group velocities and thus may contribute considerably to the thermal conductivity.</p><p>From the PDOS, it is seen that for both α-NaVO<sub>3</sub> and β-NaVO<sub>3</sub>, the low- and medium-frequency phonons below 10 THz are contributed by all atoms, while phonons above 10 THz totally originate from lighter O and V atoms. Due to the mass difference, phonon band gaps as large as 1 THz, 3.5 THz, and 3 THz in α-NaVO<sub>3</sub>, and 5 THz in β-NaVO<sub>3</sub> appear, respectively, corresponding to the discontinuous PDOS. We also observe the splitting of the peak in the PDOS for both α-NaVO<sub>3</sub> and β-NaVO<sub>3</sub>, which is an indicator of bonding heterogeneity as mentioned previously <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42 ref43 ref44" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref42 ref43 ref44" aria-label="reference 42 to 44">(42−44)</a> and is common in the vanadium–oxide materials family. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45 ref46" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref45 ref46" aria-label="reference 45 and 46">(45,46)</a> In addition, we compare the calculated heat capacities with those obtained from available experiments, <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref47" aria-label="reference 47">(47)</a> which shows a good agreement in the temperature range of 25–298 K as seen in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.jpclett.2c03061/suppl_file/jz2c03061_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S1</a>, demonstrating the reliability of our calculations. All these characteristics provide important physical insights into the unusual thermal transport in NaVO<sub>3</sub> as will be discussed later.</p><p>We first calculate the phonon transport properties of α-NaVO<sub>3</sub> using the standard anharmonic lattice dynamics based on density functional theory. As presented in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a, large amounts of vibrational modes for α-NaVO<sub>3</sub> have mean free paths along the <i>a</i>-axis smaller than the Ioffe–Regel limit even at room temperature and more so at high temperature. A similar feature is also observed in other axes, as given in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.jpclett.2c03061/suppl_file/jz2c03061_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>. This indicates that a considerable number of modes cannot be treated as normal propagating phonons, and the conventional PBTE alone cannot fully account for the phonon transport behavior of α-NaVO<sub>3</sub>. It is evident from <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>b that the phonon scattering rates at 300 K are well below the solid red line corresponding to the maximum phonon scattering rate of each mode within the phonon gas model, and with the temperature rising to 900 K, a fairly large number of modes have scattering rates higher than the solid red line. This implies the gradually decreasing dominance of propagative heat conduction with elevated temperature. Note that four-phonon scattering is not included in the present work due to the very high computational cost. However, we expect that the inclusion of four-phonon scattering would make the contribution of diffusons even more important, as the four-phonon scattering increases the overall scattering rates and consequently suppresses the contribution of propagating phonons. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48 ref49 ref50" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref48 ref49 ref50" aria-label="reference 48 to 50">(48−50)</a></p><figure id="fig2" data-index="2" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 2</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.jpclett.2c03061/asset/images/medium/jz2c03061_0002.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.jpclett.2c03061/asset/images/large/jz2c03061_0002.jpeg" id="gr2" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 2. Phonon mean free path (<i>l</i>) and scattering rate (τ<sup>–1</sup>) calculated for (a, b) α-NaVO<sub>3</sub> and (f, g) β-NaVO<sub>3</sub>. The solid red lines denote scattering rates of the phonon modes corresponding to their frequencies according to the Cahill–Watson–Pohl model. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref51" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref51" aria-label="reference 51">(51)</a> The temperature-dependent κ along <i>a</i>, <i>b</i>, and <i>c</i> axes, predicted from the unified theory for (c–e) α-NaVO<sub>3</sub> and (h–j) β-NaVO<sub>3</sub>. Herein, the contributions to the κ from normal phonons and diffuson-like phonons are separately highlighted in green and red, respectively.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.jpclett.2c03061/asset/images/large/jz2c03061_0002.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.jpclett.2c03061&amp;id=fig2"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>c–e shows the temperature-dependent lattice thermal conductivities of α-NaVO<sub>3</sub> along different axes. It is seen that normal propagative transport channels play a dominant contribution to κ over the entire temperature range, and diffusive transport channels become significant at higher temperatures. At 300 K, the predicted κ from the unified theory (<i>κ</i><sub><i>ph</i></sub> + <i>κ</i><sub><i>diff</i></sub>) is 1.31, 0.82, and 2.86 W/mK along <i>a</i>, <i>b</i>, and <i>c</i> axes, respectively, with a large anisotropy ratio of 1.60:1:3.49, where the diffusive channels make 17.1%, 20.6%, and 15.3% contributions accordingly. With the elevated temperature to 900 K, the contribution from diffusons is remarkably enhanced, which accounts from 0.29, 0.21, and 0.49 W/mK along <i>a</i>, <i>b</i>, and <i>c</i> axes, respectively. On the other hand, we also notice that the inclusion of diffusive transport channels leads to a much flatter κ dependence on <i>T</i> than the typical <i>T</i><sup>–1</sup>, especially for the <i>a</i> and <i>b</i> axes.</p><p>With the same method, we also explore the phonon transport properties of β-NaVO<sub>3</sub>. Our calculated results are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>f–j, and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.jpclett.2c03061/suppl_file/jz2c03061_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2c,d</a>. Similarly, β-NaVO<sub>3</sub> also exhibits the hierarchical heat transport behavior, where the diffusive channels have a significant contribution to κ. Remarkably, along the <i>a</i> axis, the diffusive channels even dominate the thermal transport over the propagating phonons above 600 K, resulting in the nearly independent-temperature κ. At room temperature, the unified theory predicts a large anisotropic thermal conductivity, 1:2.87:1.21, with values of 1.20, 3.44, and 1.45 W/mK along <i>a</i>, <i>b</i>, and <i>c</i> axes, respectively, which is in stark contrast to the predictions from the PBTE alone with values of 0.89, 3.18, and 1.30 W/mK along the corresponding directions. These results unambiguously reveal the crucial role of diffusive thermal transport in β-NaVO<sub>3</sub>, which even dominates the lattice thermal conductivity at high temperatures.</p><p>To gain more insight into the thermal transport of α-NaVO<sub>3</sub> and β-NaVO<sub>3</sub>, we also investigate the directional cumulative thermal conductivity contributed from both propagating and diffuson-like phonons with respect to the phonon frequency. It is apparent from <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a> that for both α-NaVO<sub>3</sub> and β-NaVO<sub>3</sub> in either direction, the normal phonon channel is mainly contributed by the low-frequency vibrational modes below 5 THz, while the diffusive channel is mainly governed by vibrational modes with frequency above 5 THz. For α-NaVO<sub>3</sub>, at <i>T</i> = 300 K, the normal phonon channel makes 85.75%, 83.39%, and 86.77% contributions to κ along <i>a</i>, <i>b</i>, and <i>c</i> axes, respectively, while the diffusive channel accounts for 14.25%, 16.61%, and 13.23% accordingly. For β-NaVO<sub>3</sub>, the normal phonon channel contributes 74.35%, 92.52%, and 89.68% to κ along <i>a</i>, <i>b</i>, and <i>c</i> axes, respectively, whereas the diffusive channel accounts for 25.65%, 7.48%, and 10.32% accordingly. Of particular note is that within the frequency range from 20 to 25 THz, the cumulative <i>κ</i><sub><i>ph</i></sub> in <i>a</i> and <i>c</i> directions for α-NaVO<sub>3</sub> shows a steep slope, indicating a remarkable contribution to <i>κ</i><sub><i>ph</i></sub> from optical phonons, i.e., 10.2% and 26.1% along <i>a</i> and <i>c</i> axes, respectively. This is striking because in general optical phonons do not contribute directly to the propagative thermal transport. By averaging the directional phonon group velocities as seen in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>a–c, we attribute this behavior to the large group velocities in that frequency region as displayed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>, parts a and c. Interestingly, this frequency region of 10–25 THz is exactly where several optical branches exhibit pronouncedly dispersive phonon curves, as marked in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>c. Hence, the direct contribution of those optical phonons to <i>κ</i><sub><i>ph</i></sub> is closely related to the nature of the complicated phonon dispersion in α-NaVO<sub>3</sub>. Similar case is also found in <i>b</i> and <i>c</i> directions for β-NaVO<sub>3</sub>, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>e–g. Besides, we also notice that the directional phonon group velocities show strong anisotropy for both α-NaVO<sub>3</sub> and β-NaVO<sub>3</sub>, consequently resulting in the large thermal conductivity anisotropy, as seen in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>, parts d and h. Importantly, stemming from the crucial diffusive thermal transport contribution, the anisotropy ratios of thermal conductivity for both α-NaVO<sub>3</sub> and β-NaVO<sub>3</sub> calculated within the PBTE scheme are obviously distinct from those obtained by the dual-phonon theory, especially at high temperatures. This finding highlights the importance of diffusive thermal transport in determining the anisotropy of thermal conductivity of α-NaVO<sub>3</sub> and β-NaVO<sub>3</sub>.</p><figure id="fig3" data-index="3" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 3</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.jpclett.2c03061/asset/images/medium/jz2c03061_0003.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.jpclett.2c03061/asset/images/large/jz2c03061_0003.jpeg" id="gr3" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 3. (a) Directional average phonon group velocities and cumulative κ with respect to the phonon frequency for (a–c) α-NaVO<sub>3</sub> and (e, f) β-NaVO<sub>3</sub> along <i>a</i>, <i>b</i>, and <i>c</i> axes at 300 K. The anisotropy ratio of κ for (d) α-NaVO<sub>3</sub> and (h) β-NaVO<sub>3</sub> calculated within the PBTE and unified theory, respectively.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.jpclett.2c03061/asset/images/large/jz2c03061_0003.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.jpclett.2c03061&amp;id=fig3"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p>For ease of comparison between α-NaVO<sub>3</sub> and β-NaVO<sub>3</sub>, <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a shows the averaged thermal conductivity of different directions as a function of temperature. It is evident that the averaged thermal conductivity of β-NaVO<sub>3</sub> is obviously higher than that of α-NaVO<sub>3</sub> in the entire temperature range, primarily due to the larger contribution of the normal phonon channel. By comparing their phonon scattering rates in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>b, we reveal that α-NaVO<sub>3</sub> having the lower thermal conductivity is mainly attributed to the stronger phonon scattering for phonons below 10 THz which have a major contribution to κ. The phonon scattering strength depends both on the phase space and anharmonicity. The phase space is a measure of the amount of available scattering channels that satisfy the energy and momentum conservation, of which the larger signifies the stronger phonon scattering and thus lower thermal conductivity. As seen in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>c, α-NaVO<sub>3</sub> has a larger phase space, especially in the frequency range below 10 THz, which is partly responsible for its lower κ. In essence, the large scattering phase space is rooted in the severe phonon branch overlap as illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>c,d, which makes the conservation rule of energy and momentum easy to satisfy for three-phonon scattering processes. Also, <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>d displays the mode-resolved Grüneisen parameter, which reflects the relative change in phonon frequency with change in crystal volume and is often regarded as a measure of phonon anharmonicity. Clearly, for the low-frequency heat-carrying phonons below 5 THz, the magnitude of Grüneisen parameter in α-NaVO<sub>3</sub> is much larger than that in β-NaVO<sub>3</sub>, demonstrating the stronger phonon anharmonicity in the former. Thus, the stronger anharmonicity of α-NaVO<sub>3</sub> in part brings in its larger phonon scattering rates and thus its lower κ. In short, the lower thermal conductivity of α-NaVO<sub>3</sub> originates from both more scattering channels and stronger phonon anharmonicity.</p><figure id="fig4" data-index="4" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 4</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.jpclett.2c03061/asset/images/medium/jz2c03061_0004.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.jpclett.2c03061/asset/images/large/jz2c03061_0004.jpeg" id="gr4" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 4. (a) Averaged κ of different directions as a function of temperature for α-NaVO<sub>3</sub> and β-NaVO<sub>3</sub>. Mode-resolved (b) three-phonon scattering rate, (c) weighted three-phonon scattering phase space, and (d) Grüneisen parameter of α-NaVO<sub>3</sub> and β-NaVO<sub>3</sub>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.jpclett.2c03061/asset/images/large/jz2c03061_0004.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.jpclett.2c03061&amp;id=fig4"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p>From the point view of practical application, grain size is a key factor affecting the thermal conductivity of NaVO<sub>3</sub>, due to a fact that most practical battery materials are made of polycrystals. This is because when the grain size is comparable to or even smaller than the phonon MFP the boundary scattering can largely diminish the propagating phonons’ contribution to the thermal conductivity. The cumulative thermal conductivity with respect to the phonon MFP can provide crucial information about the impact of grain size on thermal conductivity. As seen in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acs.jpclett.2c03061/suppl_file/jz2c03061_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>, which displays the respective cumulative <i>κ</i><sub><i>ph</i></sub> of α-NaVO<sub>3</sub> and β-NaVO<sub>3</sub>, the dominant phonon MFP in both compounds at 300 K is below 100 nm. In contrast, α-NaVO<sub>3</sub> has the dominant phonon MFP with 10 nm much shorter than that in β-NaVO<sub>3</sub>, indicating the stronger grain size effect on thermal conductivity of α-NaVO<sub>3</sub>. This information suggests that when grain size is reduced down to 100 nm, grain boundaries can strongly affect the thermal conductivity of NaVO<sub>3</sub> polycrystals.</p><p>The above results reveal that the hierarchical thermal transport feature in NaVO<sub>3</sub> is mainly caused by a large number of diffuson-like phonons, which is largely a result of strong phonon anharmonicity as suggested by the large Grüneisen parameter. To further explore the origin of strong phonon anharmonicity in NaVO<sub>3</sub>, the bond lengths between different atomic species, the projected crystal orbital Hamilton population (pCOHP), and electron localization function (ELF) are analyzed, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>. The coordination environments around Na and V atoms for α-NaVO<sub>3</sub> and β-NaVO<sub>3</sub> are plotted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>a–d, respectively. The V atom is coordinated by four/five O atoms for α-NaVO<sub>3</sub>/β-NaVO<sub>3</sub>, and the Na atom is octahedrally coordinated by six O atoms for both two compounds. Clearly, the interatomic distances between V and O for α-NaVO<sub>3</sub> are much shorter overall than those for β-NaVO<sub>3</sub>, reflecting the stronger V–O bond strength in α-NaVO<sub>3</sub> than β-NaVO<sub>3</sub>, which coincides with the stronger phonon anharmonicity in α-NaVO<sub>3</sub>. Furthermore, the bond length between Na/V and O atoms varies significantly, indicating the remarkable bonding heterogeneity. This bonding heterogeneity can be further understood by the negative of pCOHP (−pCOHP), which can measure the bonding strength between atom pairs through analyzing the bonding and antibonding states below Fermi level. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref52 ref53" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref52 ref53" aria-label="reference 52 and 53">(52,53)</a> The positive value of −pCOHP represents bonding interactions and lowers the system energy; it otherwise represents antibonding interactions and increases the system energy. Generally, the larger magnitude of −pCOHP, the stronger bonding interaction. It can be clearly seen in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>e–h that the −pCOHP shows a significant difference of bonding environment among the nearest Na/V–O atoms, implying a diversity of atomic bonding strengths in these compounds. In comparison, the V–O bonds have the absolute values of −pCOHP several times larger than those of Na–O bonds, indicating the much weaker atomic interaction between Na–O atoms for these two compounds. It suggests that the Na and O atoms are loosely bonded together, probably causing the Na atoms to have relatively large thermal displacement, which can contribute substantially to phonon anharmonicity. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21 ref54 ref55" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref21 ref54 ref55" aria-label="reference 21, 54 and 55">(21,54,55)</a></p><figure id="fig5" data-index="5" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 5</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acs.jpclett.2c03061/asset/images/medium/jz2c03061_0005.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acs.jpclett.2c03061/asset/images/large/jz2c03061_0005.jpeg" id="gr5" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 5. Coordination environments around V and Na atoms for (a, b) α-NaVO<sub>3</sub> and (c, d) β-NaVO<sub>3</sub>. The negative projected crystal orbital Hamilton population (−pCOHP) curves of Na–O and V–O interactions for (e, f) α-NaVO<sub>3</sub> and (g, h) β-NaVO<sub>3</sub>. Slice projection along (001) plane (down) of electron localization function (ELF) for (i) α-NaVO<sub>3</sub> and (j) β-NaVO<sub>3</sub>.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acs.jpclett.2c03061/asset/images/large/jz2c03061_0005.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acs.jpclett.2c03061&amp;id=fig5"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><p>To further characterize the strength of atomic bonding between different atomic species, parts i and j of <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a> also plot the (001) plane of ELF distribution for α-NaVO<sub>3</sub> and β-NaVO<sub>3</sub>, respectively. ELF varies from 0 to 1, where 0, 0.5, and 1.0 represent nonlocalized electrons, fully delocalized electrons, and perfectly localized electrons, respectively. The larger the ELF value is, the higher the electron localization degree is, and the stronger the atomic interactions. It is obvious that for both compounds the ELF around O and V atoms is almost identical with the magnitude of ∼0.5, indicating that V atoms share equally electrons with neighboring O atoms and thus covalently connect onto the adjacent O atoms. Comparably, the ELF around Na atoms is much lower with the value below ∼0.2, indicating the electron transfer from Na atoms to the adjacent O atoms and thus ionic bonds between Na atoms with neighboring O atoms. This suggests that the large phonon anharmonicity in NaVO<sub>3</sub> may be derived from the 2p<sup>6</sup> lone electron pair of Na<sup>+</sup>, which makes Na atoms more prone to deformation by lattice vibration. <a href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21 ref56" id="referenceLink" data-rid="ref21 ref56" aria-label="reference 21 and 56">(21,56)</a> From this point, we judge that the thermal conductivity of NaVO<sub>3</sub> is strongly dependent on the sodium concentration, which inspires us to tune the thermal conductivity of electrode materials for SIBs by controlling the Na content or to use the thermal conductivity as an indicator for sodium detection.</p><p class="last">In summary, we have disclosed the hierarchical thermal transport behavior in NaVO<sub>3</sub> by using the state-of-the-art unified theory. After considering the contributions from both particle-like and wave-like phonon channels, we predict that α-NaVO<sub>3</sub> and β-NaVO<sub>3</sub> have anisotropic and ultralow lattice thermal conductivities. Moreover, we find that the diffusive phonon transport channels make a significant contribution to the thermal conductivity of NaVO<sub>3</sub> even at room temperature, and more so at higher temperatures, which leads to the breakdown of conventional PBTE theory. Further lattice structure, pCOHP, and ELF analyses demonstrate that the hierarchical thermal transport of NaVO<sub>3</sub> arises from the chemical bonding heterogeneity caused by the weak Na–O bonds and strong V–O bonds, which implies the strong dependence of thermal conductivity on the Na concentration. These findings provide a novel insight for the fundamental thermal physics of sodium-intercalated layered metal oxides, and they are expected to stimulate future experimental exploration on their thermal transport for the development of next generation batteries.</p><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div This review describes some of the exciting progress being made in this area through use of computer simulation techniques, focusing primarily on pos. electrode (cathode) materials for lithium-ion batteries, but also including a timely overview of the growing area of new cathode materials for sodium-ion batteries.         ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">0306-0012</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Royal Society of Chemistry</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Energy prodn. and storage technologies have attracted a great deal of attention for day-to-day applications.  In recent decades, advances in lithium-ion battery (LIB) technol. have improved living conditions around the globe.  LIBs are used in most mobile electronic devices as well as in zero-emission electronic vehicles.  However, there are increasing concerns regarding load leveling of renewable energy sources and the smart grid as well as the sustainability of lithium sources due to their limited availability and consequent expected price increase.  Therefore, whether LIBs alone can satisfy the rising demand for small- and/or mid-to-large-format energy storage applications remains unclear.  To mitigate these issues, recent research has focused on alternative energy storage systems.  Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are considered as the best candidate power sources because sodium is widely available and exhibits similar chem. to that of LIBs; therefore, SIBs are promising next-generation alternatives.  Recently, sodiated layer transition metal oxides, phosphates and org. compds. have been introduced as cathode materials for SIBs.  Simultaneously, recent developments have been facilitated by the use of select carbonaceous materials, transition metal oxides (or sulfides), and intermetallic and org. compds. as anodes for SIBs.  Apart from electrode materials, suitable electrolytes, additives, and binders are equally important for the development of practical SIBs.  Despite developments in electrode materials and other components, there remain several challenges, including cell design and electrode balancing, in the application of sodium ion cells.  In this article, we summarize and discuss current research on materials and propose future directions for SIBs.  This will provide important insights into scientific and practical issues in the development of SIBs.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref4"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref4" class="refNumLink">4</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit4" data-doi="10.1021/acs.chemmater.0c02244"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Su, B.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wu, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liang, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, W.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Liu, J.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Goonetilleke, D.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sharma, N.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Sit, P.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries (NIBs) are a potential low-cost alternative to lithium-ion batteries for large-scale energy storage, but many high-capacity NIB cathode materials undergo irreversible structural changes during charge and discharge, leading to fast capacity fading.  Herein, monoclinic NaVO3 exhibits good cycle performance with high capacity as a cathode material for NIBs.  In situ synchrotron X-ray diffraction studies show that the material structure is virtually invariant during Na+ (de-)intercalation, with the a and b lattice parameters changing only by 0.13 and 0.19%, resp.  The material undergoes an oxygen redox reaction during initial charge while delivering a remarkable specific capacity of 245 mAh g-1 (1.2-4.7 V) with contributions from cationic (V4+/V5+) and anionic (O2-/O-) redox couples during discharge.  The stable VO4 tetrahedral framework also enables the material to give superior rate and cycle capabilities, with a capacity of 164 mAh g-1 (67% utilization) at a current of 1000 mA g-1 (about 5C) and a capacity retention of 90% after 50 cycles.  D. functional theory calcns. further verify the stability of the material and the charge-discharge mechanism.  This work can broaden the horizon for designing high-energy cathode materials with enhanced structural stability for sodium-ion batteries.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref5"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref5" class="refNumLink">5</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit5" data-doi="10.1016/j.jpowsour.2021.230535"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Dong, Y.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Yuan, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Zhou, S.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Tan, H.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ma, Z.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wang, X.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Xia, F.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Wong, C.-P.</span></span> <span> </span><span class="NLM_article-title">Electrochemically incorporated Cu nanoparticles in NaVO<sub>3</sub> with enhanced sodium-ion battery performance</span>. <i>J.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) are believed to be the promising low-cost candidates as compared to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs).  Particularly, layered metal oxides show great potential as electrode materials with high capacity for SIB.  However, the layered metal oxides undergo irreversible structural changes and suffer from low cond. during charge and discharge, leading to sluggish electrochem. processes and fast capacity fading.  To overcome these weaknesses, we electrochem. in-situ incorporate copper nanoparticles in NaVO3 host material during battery cycling processes, giving rise to fast electron transfer in the material matrix.  The resultant copper-incorporated NaVO3 is demonstrated to have significantly increased performance for SIBs, which exhibits excellent reversible capacity, high coulombic efficiency, large capacity retention and outstanding rate capability.  From ex-situ microscopy and spectroscopy characterizations, we show the evidence of the electrochem. formed Cu nanoparticles.  This strategy also provides a general method to form homogeneous metal incorporation and improve the electrochem. properties for low-cond. electrode materials.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref6"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref6" class="refNumLink">6</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit6" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.8b03571"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Ali, G.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Islam, M.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Jung, H. Their measurements reveal that the highest in-plane and cross-plane thermal conductivities achieved in the carbon-nanotube-enhanced electrodes reached up to 141 and 3.6 W/mK, resp.         </div><div class="casAbstract">Understanding thermal transport in Li intercalated layered materials is not only important for managing heat generation and dissipation in lithium ion batteries but also the understanding potentially provides a novel way to design materials with reversibly tunable thermal cond. We found that intercalation of Li has an anisotropic effect on tuning the thermal cond.: the thermal cond. in the basal plane decreases monotonically from 1232 W/m-K of pristine graphite to 444 W/m-K of the fully lithiated LiC6, while the thermal cond. along the c-axis decreases 1st from 6.5 W/m-K for graphite to 1.3 W/m-K for LiC18 and then increases to 5.0 W/m-K for LiC6 as the Li compn. increases. The intercalated Li ions are found to suppress the phonon lifetime and to reduce the group velocity of phonons parallel to the basal plane but significantly to increase the phonon group velocity along the c-axis, which anisotropically tunes the thermal cond. of lithiated graphite compds.         </div><div class="casAbstract">The development of battery sensing techniques is crucial for safe, reliable, and fast operation of lithium-ion batteries (LIBs).  There is a growing realization that the spatially averaged chem. information provided by existing battery diagnostic tools is insufficient for understanding degrdn. of LIBs.  Here, we report the use of thermal waves for operando probing of the local lithium concn. as a function of depth inside battery electrodes.                         cas_cit19R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">19</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">Thermal conductivity of intercalation, conversion, and alloying lithium-ion battery electrode materials as function of their state of charge</span></div><div class="casAuthors">Shin, Jungwoo; Kim, Sanghyeon; Park, Hoonkee; Won Jang, Ho; Cahill, David G.; Braun, Paul V.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Current Opinion in Solid State &amp; Materials Science</span>

            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">Elsevier Ltd.</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Upon insertion and extn. of lithium, materials important for electrochem. energy storage can undergo changes in thermal cond. (Λ) and elastic modulus (M).  These changes are attributed to evolution of the intrinsic thermal carrier lifetime and interat. bonding strength assocd. with structural transitions of electrode materials with varying degrees of reversibility.  Using in situ time-domain thermoreflectance (TDTR) and picosecond acoustics, we systemically study Λ and M of conversion, intercalation and alloying electrode materials during cycling.  The intercalation V2O5 and TiO2 exhibit non-monotonic reversible Λ and M switching up to a factor of 1.8 (Λ) and 1.5 (M) as a function of lithium content.  The conversion Fe2O3 and NiO undergo irreversible decays in Λ and M upon the first lithiation.  The alloying Sb shows the largest and partially reversible order of the magnitude switching in Λ between the delithiated (18 W m-1 K-1) and lithiated states (&lt;1 W m-1 K-1).  The irreversible Λ is attributed to structural degrdn. and pulverization resulting from substantial vol. changes during cycling. B 34, 5058(1986)], finds large deviations from the Eucken-Debye result κ(T)=A/T predicted by the phonon-gas model, even though phonon quasiparticles are fairly well defined. Jeffrey; Hermann, Raphael P.</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">Materials Today Physics</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2021</span>),

        </div><div class="casAbstract">The physics of heat conduction puts practical limits on many technol. fields such as energy prodn., storage, and conversion.  It is now widely appreciated that the phonon-gas model does not describe the full vibrational spectrum in amorphous materials, since this picture likely breaks down at higher frequencies.  A two-channel heat conduction model, which uses harmonic vibrational states and lattice dynamics as a basis, has recently been shown to capture both crystal-like (phonon-gas channel) and amorphous-like (diffuson channel) heat conduction.  While materials design principles for the phonon-gas channel are well established, similar understanding and control of the diffuson channel is lacking.  In this work, in order to uncover design principles for the diffuson channel, we study structurally-complex cryst. Yb14 (Mn,Mg)Sb11, a champion thermoelec. material above 800 K, exptl. using inelastic neutron scattering and computationally using the two-channel lattice dynamical approach.  Our results show that the diffuson channel indeed dominates in Yb14MnSb11 above 300 K.  More importantly, we demonstrate a method for the rational design of amorphous-like heat conduction by considering the energetic proximity phonon modes and modifying them through chem. means.  We show that increasing (decreasing) the mass on the Sb-site decreases (increases) the energy of these modes such that there is greater (smaller) overlap with Yb-dominated modes resulting in a higher (lower) thermal cond.  This design strategy is exactly opposite of what is expected when the phonon-gas channel and/or common anal. models for the diffuson channel are considered, since in both cases an increase in at. mass commonly leads to a decrease in thermal cond.  This work demonstrates how two-channel lattice dynamics can not only quant. predict the relative importance of the phonon-gas and diffuson channels, but also lead to rational design strategies in materials where the diffuson channel is important.</div></div></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref26"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" data-citation="" data-rid="ref26" class="refNumLink">26</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit26" data-doi="10.1063/5.0055593"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Hanus, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Gurunathan, R.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lindsay, L.</span></span>; <span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Agne, M.
